Scope ALL keyword_search/nls_search queries to these repos.
Use the repo name as the `repo` parameter for read_file/go_to_definition/find_references.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Onboarding: Cilium Codebase Orientation

**Repository:** github.com/sg-evals/cilium--v1.16.5 (mirror of cilium/cilium)
**Task Type:** Codebase Orientation (analysis only — no code changes)

## Scenario

You are a new engineer joining a team that works on Cilium, a cloud-native networking, observability, and security platform that uses eBPF. Your manager has asked you to spend your first day exploring the codebase and answering key orientation questions so you can hit the ground running.

## Your Task

Explore the Cilium codebase and answer the following questions. Write your answers to `/logs/agent/onboarding.md`.

### Questions

1. **Main Entry Point**: Where does the cilium-agent binary start execution? Identify the main function, how the CLI is initialized, and what dependency injection framework is used to wire components together.

2. **Core Packages**: Identify at least 5 core packages under `pkg/` and describe what each one is responsible for. Focus on the packages that handle networking policy, the datapath, Kubernetes integration, endpoint management, and eBPF maps.

3. **Configuration Loading**: How does the agent load its configuration? Describe the configuration pipeline: what config formats are supported, what library is used for config binding, and What modules/interfaces define the main config struct?

4. **Test Structure**: How are tests organized in this project? Describe at least 3 different testing approaches used (e.g., unit tests, integration tests, privileged tests, BPF tests). Where do end-to-end tests live?

5. **Network Policy Pipeline**: Trace the path of a CiliumNetworkPolicy from CRD definition to eBPF enforcement. Identify at least 4 stages in this pipeline and name the relevant components or packages involved at each stage (e.g., CRD types, K8s watcher, policy repository, endpoint regeneration, BPF map sync).

6. **Adding a New Network Policy Type**: If you needed to add a new type of network policy rule (e.g., a new L7 protocol filter), which packages and files would you need to modify? Describe the sequence of changes required.

## Output Requirements

Write your answers to `/logs/agent/onboarding.md` with this structure:

```
# Cilium Codebase Orientation

## 1. Main Entry Point
<Your answer>

## 2. Core Packages
<Your answer>

## 3. Configuration Loading
<Your answer>

## 4. Test Structure
<Your answer>

## 5. Network Policy Pipeline
<Your answer>

## 6. Adding a New Network Policy Type
<Your answer>
```

## Constraints

- Do NOT modify any source files
- Do NOT write any code changes
- Your job is exploration and documentation only
- Be specific — include file paths, package names, function names, and struct names where relevant

117-assistanttext-
The onboarding document has been written to `/logs/agent/onboarding.md`. Here's a summary of what was covered:

---

**1. Main Entry Point**
- `daemon/main.go:11` — `func main()` creates a `hive.New(cmd.Agent)` and runs it via `cmd.Execute(cmd.NewAgentCmd(agentHive))`
- CLI built with **Cobra**, DI wiring via **Hive** (`github.com/cilium/hive`) — a cell-based framework similar to `uber-go/fx`
- The `Agent` cell in `daemon/cmd/cells.go` composes `Infrastructure`, `ControlPlane`, and `datapath.Cell`

**2. Core Packages**
- `pkg/policy` — policy `Repository`, `SelectorCache`, `PolicyCache`, and the `api/` sub-package for rule types
- `pkg/datapath` — abstracts kernel operations; `loader/` compiles/loads BPF programs; `types/` defines interfaces
- `pkg/k8s` — Kubernetes clients, CRD types (`cnp_types.go`), watchers, resource stores
- `pkg/endpoint` — `Endpoint` struct, `regeneratePolicy()`, `regenerateBPF()`, `syncPolicyMap()`
- `pkg/maps` — BPF map schemas: `policymap`, `ctmap`, `lxcmap`, `ipcache`, `lbmap`, `nat`

**3. Configuration Loading**
- Viper + Cobra pipeline: flag registration → `cobra.OnInitialize` hooks → `option.InitConfig()` (reads config dir + YAML/TOML/JSON file) → populates `option.DaemonConfig` global

**4. Test Structure**
- Standard unit tests, privileged tests (`testutils.PrivilegedTest`), BPF C tests in `bpf/tests/`, Ginkgo e2e tests in `test/`

**5. Network Policy Pipeline**
- CRD types → K8s watcher (`pkg/policy/k8s/`) → `Repository` → `regeneratePolicy()` → `regenerateBPF()` + `syncPolicyMap()` → kernel BPF `policymap`

**6. Adding a New Policy Type**
- 7-step sequence: API struct → CRD validation → rule validation → internal L4 model → proxy handling → BPF header → tests

{
  "content": "# Cilium Codebase Orientation\n\n## 1. Main Entry Point\n\nThe `cilium-agent` binary starts in `daemon/main.go`:\n\n```go\nfunc main() {\n    agentHive := hive.New(cmd.Agent)\n    cmd.Execute(cmd.NewAgentCmd(agentHive))\n}\n```\n\n**Binary entry point:** `daemon/main.go:11` \u2014 `func main()`\n\n**CLI initialization** is handled in `daemon/cmd/root.go` via `NewAgentCmd()`. This function:\n- Creates a `cobra.Command` named `\"cilium-agent\"`\n- Registers all CLI flags via `h.RegisterFlags(rootCmd.Flags())` and `InitGlobalFlags()`\n- Registers cobra init hooks: `option.InitConfig()`, `initDaemonConfig()`, `initLogging()`\n- The command's `Run` function calls `initEnv()`, validates config, and then `h.Run()` to start the hive\n\n**Dependency Injection Framework:** Cilium uses **Hive** (`github.com/cilium/hive`), a custom DI framework based on `uber-go/fx`-style cells. The `hive.New()` call in `daemon/main.go` takes a root `cell.Cell` \u2014 `cmd.Agent` \u2014 and wires all components together.\n\n**Component composition** is defined in `daemon/cmd/cells.go`. The `Agent` cell is a `cell.Module` that contains:\n- `Infrastructure` \u2014 external access (Kubernetes client, pprof, gops, metrics server, Cilium REST API)\n- `ControlPlane` \u2014 per-node control logic (endpoint manager, node manager, policy watcher, IPAM, services, etc.)\n- `datapath.Cell` \u2014 privileged kernel-level operations (BPF maps, iptables, loader, ipcache sync)\n\nEach sub-cell provides or consumes typed dependencies declared via `cell.Provide`, `cell.Invoke`, `cell.Config`, and struct tags (`cell.In`).\n\n---\n\n## 2. Core Packages\n\n### `pkg/policy` \u2014 Network Policy Engine\nThe central policy package. Key types:\n- `Repository` (`pkg/policy/repository.go:110`) \u2014 stores all active policy rules, indexed by `ruleKey` and `ResourceID`. Exposes two event queues: `RepositoryChangeQueue` and `RuleReactionQueue` to serialize policy updates and reactions (endpoint regenerations).\n- `SelectorCache` (`pkg/policy/selectorcache.go`) \u2014 caches label selectors to avoid repeated evaluation.\n- `PolicyCache` / `SelectorPolicy` (`pkg/policy/distillery.go`) \u2014 caches per-identity resolved policies ready for datapath consumption.\n- The `api` sub-package (`pkg/policy/api/`) defines the user-facing rule structures (`Rule`, `IngressRule`, `EgressRule`, `PortRule`, `L7Rules`, etc.).\n\n### `pkg/datapath` \u2014 Datapath Abstraction\nAbstracts all kernel-level operations. Key sub-packages:\n- `pkg/datapath/types/` \u2014 interfaces (`Datapath`, `Loader`, `NodeHandler`, `ConfigWriter`, etc.) that decouple control-plane from kernel implementation.\n- `pkg/datapath/linux/` \u2014 Linux-specific implementation (TC/XDP program attachment, routing, neighbors).\n- `pkg/datapath/loader/` \u2014 compiles and loads BPF programs per endpoint using clang.\n- `pkg/datapath/ipcache/` \u2014 synchronizes the userspace IPCache with the kernel BPF `ipcache` map.\n- `pkg/datapath/cells.go` \u2014 the hive cell that wires together all datapath sub-components.\n\n### `pkg/k8s` \u2014 Kubernetes Integration\nEverything needed to interact with the Kubernetes API:\n- `pkg/k8s/client/` \u2014 provides `Clientset` (typed K8s client), injected via hive.\n- `pkg/k8s/apis/cilium.io/v2/` \u2014 CRD Go types: `CiliumNetworkPolicy` (`cnp_types.go`), `CiliumClusterwideNetworkPolicy` (`ccnp_types.go`), `CiliumEndpoint`, `CiliumNode`, etc.\n- `pkg/k8s/watchers/` \u2014 the core K8s watchers cell (pod, node, service, endpoint watchers).\n- `pkg/k8s/resource/` \u2014 typed resource stores and event streams over K8s objects.\n- `pkg/k8s/synced/` \u2014 coordination primitives to wait until specific CRDs/resources are synced before proceeding.\n\n### `pkg/endpoint` \u2014 Endpoint Management\nModels a single local workload endpoint (pod/container):\n- `Endpoint` struct (`pkg/endpoint/endpoint.go`) \u2014 tracks all state: identity, labels, policy, BPF maps, proxy config.\n- `pkg/endpoint/policy.go` \u2014 `regeneratePolicy()` recomputes policy for an endpoint from the repository.\n- `pkg/endpoint/bpf.go` \u2014 `regenerateBPF()` rewrites BPF headers and syncs BPF maps; `syncPolicyMap()` applies pending policy map changes to the kernel `policymap`.\n- `pkg/endpoint/regeneration/` \u2014 context types and triggers for endpoint regeneration.\n\n### `pkg/maps` \u2014 eBPF Map Definitions\nDefines all BPF map schemas and Go accessors:\n- `pkg/maps/policymap/` \u2014 the per-endpoint policy map (`PolicyMap`) used by the BPF datapath to make allow/deny decisions.\n- `pkg/maps/ctmap/` \u2014 connection tracking maps.\n- `pkg/maps/lxcmap/` \u2014 the endpoint (LXC) map mapping endpoint IDs to their metadata.\n- `pkg/maps/nat/` \u2014 NAT maps for SNAT/DNAT.\n- `pkg/maps/ipcache/` \u2014 the global ipcache BPF map mapping IPs to security identities.\n- `pkg/maps/lbmap/` \u2014 load balancer maps for service VIP routing.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Configuration Loading\n\nConfiguration is loaded through a multi-stage pipeline using **Viper** (`github.com/spf13/viper`) as the config-binding library.\n\n**Pipeline stages (in order):**\n\n1. **Flag registration** \u2014 `InitGlobalFlags()` in `daemon/cmd/daemon_main.go` registers all CLI flags with Cobra and binds each flag to a Viper key via `option.BindEnv()` (`pkg/option/config.go:1361`). This also binds the `CILIUM_<FLAG>` environment variable for every flag.\n\n2. **cobra.OnInitialize hooks** \u2014 registered in `daemon/cmd/root.go:66`, three hooks run before the command:\n   - `option.InitConfig()` (`pkg/option/config.go:4215`) \u2014 reads config from the `--config-dir` directory (a directory of key=value files, similar to a Kubernetes ConfigMap mount), then reads a YAML/TOML/JSON config file specified via `--config`. Uses `vp.ReadInConfig()` to parse it.\n   - `initDaemonConfig()` \u2014 populates the global `option.Config` (`*option.DaemonConfig`) from the populated Viper instance.\n   - `initLogging()` \u2014 configures log levels and output.\n\n3. **Config formats supported:** YAML, TOML, JSON (via Viper), plus directory-based key=value config files (like K8s ConfigMaps).\n\n4. **Main config struct:** `option.DaemonConfig` (`pkg/option/config.go:1401`) \u2014 a large struct with hundreds of fields covering all daemon options. The global singleton is `option.Config`. Cells that need config can depend on `*option.DaemonConfig` directly (provided via `cell.Provide(func() *option.DaemonConfig { return option.Config })` in `daemon/cmd/cells.go:101`).\n\n5. **Per-cell config:** Individual cells also define their own small config structs using `cell.Config(MyConfig{})`, which are automatically populated from Viper by the hive framework using struct field tags.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Test Structure\n\nThe project uses several distinct testing approaches:\n\n### 1. Standard Go Unit Tests (`_test.go`)\nCo-located with the package they test (e.g., `pkg/policy/repository_test.go`, `pkg/option/config_test.go`). Run with `go test`. These tests use the standard `testing` package and often `github.com/stretchr/testify`. They test pure logic without kernel dependencies.\n\n### 2. Privileged Tests (`PrivilegedTest`)\nTests that require root/kernel capabilities (e.g., loading BPF programs, creating network namespaces). They call `testutils.PrivilegedTest(t)` at the start, which skips the test when not running as root. Examples:\n- `pkg/maps/policymap/policymap_privileged_test.go` \u2014 tests actual BPF map operations.\n- `pkg/datapath/loader/tc_test.go` \u2014 tests TC program attachment.\n- `pkg/datapath/linux/probes/probes_test.go` \u2014 tests kernel feature probes.\n- `pkg/mountinfo/mountinfo_privileged_test.go` \u2014 tests filesystem mount operations.\n\n### 3. BPF / Datapath C Tests (`bpf/tests/`)\nWritten in C and run using a custom Go test harness (`bpf/tests/bpftest/bpf_test.go`). These tests compile and execute eBPF programs in a controlled environment to test the datapath logic in isolation. Examples: `bpf/tests/tc_lxc_policy_drop.c`, `bpf/tests/bpf_ct_tests.c`, `bpf/tests/conntrack_test.c`.\n\n### 4. End-to-End / Integration Tests (`test/`)\nThe `test/` directory contains Ginkgo-based e2e tests that run against real Kubernetes clusters (or VMs via Vagrant):\n- Entry point: `test/test_suite_test.go` \u2014 bootstraps the Ginkgo suite, imports `test/k8s` and `test/runtime`.\n- `test/k8s/` \u2014 Kubernetes e2e tests: network policies (`net_policies.go`), services, Hubble, FQDN, BGP, etc.\n- `test/runtime/` \u2014 runtime tests against a single node.\n- `test/controlplane/` \u2014 controlplane integration tests (lighter-weight, testing policy and endpoint reconciliation without a full cluster).\n- Uses `github.com/onsi/ginkgo` and `github.com/onsi/gomega`.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Network Policy Pipeline\n\nA `CiliumNetworkPolicy` travels through the following stages from CRD definition to eBPF enforcement:\n\n### Stage 1: CRD Type Definition\n**Package:** `pkg/k8s/apis/cilium.io/v2/`\n**Key file:** `cnp_types.go`\nThe `CiliumNetworkPolicy` struct wraps `api.Rule` (from `pkg/policy/api/rule.go`) as its `Spec` field. The `Rule` struct contains `EndpointSelector`, `Ingress []IngressRule`, `Egress []EgressRule`, etc. Rules reference port/protocol combinations (`PortRule`, `PortRuleL7`) and selectors.\n\n### Stage 2: Kubernetes Watcher / Event Ingestion\n**Package:** `pkg/policy/k8s/`\n**Key files:** `watcher.go`, `cilium_network_policy.go`\nThe `policyWatcher` struct (registered via `policyK8s.Cell` in `daemon/cmd/cells.go:227`) watches K8s resource streams for `CiliumNetworkPolicy`, `CiliumClusterwideNetworkPolicy`, `NetworkPolicy`, and `CiliumCIDRGroup` resources. On `Upsert` events it calls `onUpsert()` which translates K8s objects into internal policy representations and calls the `PolicyManager` interface.\n\n### Stage 3: Policy Repository\n**Package:** `pkg/policy/`\n**Key file:** `repository.go`\nThe `Repository` struct holds all active policy rules in a `map[ruleKey]*rule`. Updates are serialized through `RepositoryChangeQueue`. When rules are added/removed, the repository increments its `revision` counter and queues endpoint regeneration events through `RuleReactionQueue`.\n\n### Stage 4: Endpoint Policy Regeneration\n**Package:** `pkg/endpoint/`\n**Key files:** `policy.go`, `bpf.go`\nWhen an endpoint's policy changes (new CNP applied, identity changed), `regeneratePolicy()` (`pkg/endpoint/policy.go:200`) is called. It consults the `PolicyCache` / `SelectorPolicy` (`pkg/policy/distillery.go`) to compute the resolved `EndpointPolicy` \u2014 a distilled set of allow/deny rules for this specific endpoint's identity. This produces a `policyGenerateResult` containing changes to apply.\n\nThen `regenerateBPF()` (`pkg/endpoint/bpf.go:520`) is called. It:\n- Rewrites BPF header files with the endpoint's configuration\n- Calls the datapath loader to recompile and reload BPF programs for the endpoint\n- Calls `syncPolicyMap()` to apply changes to the kernel BPF policy map\n\n### Stage 5: BPF Policy Map Sync\n**Package:** `pkg/endpoint/`, `pkg/maps/policymap/`\n**Key files:** `pkg/endpoint/bpf.go:1333` (`syncPolicyMap()`), `pkg/maps/policymap/policymap.go`\n`syncPolicyMap()` reconciles the desired policy state (computed in stage 4) against the actual kernel BPF `PolicyMap` for the endpoint. It adds/removes entries in the per-endpoint `policymap` BPF map (path: `bpf/LocalMapPath(policymap.MapName, endpointID)`). The BPF datapath then uses this map to make real-time allow/deny decisions for every packet.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Adding a New Network Policy Type\n\nIf you needed to add a new L7 protocol filter (e.g., a new \"MyProtocol\" L7 filter), here is the sequence of changes:\n\n### Step 1: Define the API type\n**Package:** `pkg/policy/api/`\n- Add a new struct `PortRuleMyProtocol` in a new file `pkg/policy/api/myprotocol.go` (following the pattern of `pkg/policy/api/http.go` or `pkg/policy/api/kafka.go` from the proxy package).\n- Add the field to `PortRule` in `pkg/policy/api/l4.go` (e.g., `MyProtocol *PortRuleMyProtocol`).\n- Run `zz_generated.deepcopy.go` regeneration.\n\n### Step 2: Update the CRD type validation\n**Package:** `pkg/k8s/apis/cilium.io/v2/`\n- Add kubebuilder validation annotations if needed.\n- Regenerate the CRD JSON schema (`zz_generated.deepcopy.go`, CRD YAML under `pkg/k8s/apis/cilium.io/client/`).\n\n### Step 3: Add rule validation\n**Package:** `pkg/policy/api/`\n- Add validation logic in `rule_validation.go` for the new `PortRuleMyProtocol`.\n\n### Step 4: Translate to internal representation\n**Package:** `pkg/policy/`\n- In `pkg/policy/l4.go`, extend `L4Filter` and the rule resolution logic to handle the new protocol type.\n- Update `rule.go` in the policy package to process the new rule in `resolveIngressPolicy()` / `resolveEgressPolicy()`.\n\n### Step 5: Implement proxy redirection\n**Package:** `pkg/proxy/`\n- Add a new proxy type or extend an existing proxy (Envoy or DNS proxy) to handle the new protocol.\n- Register the new parser/handler.\n\n### Step 6: Wire into the Envoy/proxy layer (if L7)\n**Package:** `pkg/envoy/` or `pkg/proxy/`\n- Add xDS configuration generation for the new protocol if it routes through Envoy.\n- Update `pkg/policy/repository.go`'s `GetEnvoyHTTPRules` equivalent for the new protocol.\n\n### Step 7: Update endpoint regeneration\n**Package:** `pkg/endpoint/`\n- Ensure `regeneratePolicy()` correctly computes the new L7 redirect information.\n- Ensure `regenerateBPF()` generates the correct BPF header flags to redirect traffic to the proxy.\n\n### Step 8: Add tests\n- Unit tests for the new `PortRuleMyProtocol` validation in `pkg/policy/api/`.\n- Policy resolution tests in `pkg/policy/` (e.g., `l4_test.go`, `rule_test.go`).\n- Integration tests in `test/k8s/` if applicable.\n",

Network Policy Pipeline\n\nA `CiliumNetworkPolicy` travels through the following stages from CRD definition to eBPF enforcement:\n\n### Stage 1: CRD Type Definition\n**Package:** `pkg/k8s/apis/cilium.io/v2/`\n**Key file:** `cnp_types.go`\n\n`CiliumNetworkPolicy` is a Kubernetes CRD with `Spec *api.Rule` and `Specs api.Rules`. The `api.Rule` struct (from `pkg/policy/api/rule.go`) contains `EndpointSelector`, `Ingress []IngressRule`, `Egress []EgressRule`, and optional `EnableDefaultDeny`. `IngressRule` and `EgressRule` compose `IngressCommonRule`/`EgressCommonRule` (endpoint/CIDR/entity selectors) with `ToPorts []PortRule` (L4/L7 rules).\n\n### Stage 2: Kubernetes Watcher / Event Ingestion\n**Package:** `pkg/policy/k8s/`\n**Key files:** `watcher.go`, `cilium_network_policy.go`\n\nThe `policyWatcher` struct (registered via `policyK8s.Cell` in `daemon/cmd/cells.go`) watches typed K8s resource event streams for `CiliumNetworkPolicy`, `CiliumClusterwideNetworkPolicy`, `NetworkPolicy`, and `CiliumCIDRGroup` resources. On `Upsert` events, `onUpsert()` translates the K8s object to an internal `types.SlimCNP`, resolves any `CiliumCIDRGroup` references, and calls the `PolicyManager` interface to add rules to the repository.\n\n### Stage 3: Policy Repository\n**Package:** `pkg/policy/`\n**Key file:** `repository.go`\n\nThe `Repository` struct holds all active rules in `map[ruleKey]*rule`. Updates are serialized through `RepositoryChangeQueue`. When rules change, the repository increments its `revision` atomic counter and queues endpoint regeneration events through `RuleReactionQueue`, which triggers all affected endpoints to recompute their policy.\n\n### Stage 4: Endpoint Policy Computation\n**Package:** `pkg/endpoint/`, `pkg/policy/`\n**Key files:** `pkg/endpoint/policy.go`, `pkg/policy/distillery.go`\n\n`regeneratePolicy()` (`pkg/endpoint/policy.go:200`) is called for each affected endpoint. It uses the `PolicyCache` (`pkg/policy/distillery.go`) to resolve the endpoint's identity against all repository rules via `SelectorPolicy.Consume()`, producing an `EndpointPolicy` \u2014 a distilled, identity-indexed set of allow/deny rules specific to this endpoint.\n\n### Stage 5: BPF Regeneration and Policy Map Sync\n**Package:** `pkg/endpoint/`, `pkg/maps/policymap/`\n**Key files:** `pkg/endpoint/bpf.go`, `pkg/maps/policymap/policymap.go`\n\n`regenerateBPF()` (`pkg/endpoint/bpf.go:520`) rewrites BPF C header files for the endpoint and instructs the datapath loader to recompile and reload the endpoint's BPF programs. Then `syncPolicyMap()` (`pkg/endpoint/bpf.go:1333`) reconciles the desired `EndpointPolicy` against the live kernel `PolicyMap` BPF map (one per endpoint, at path `bpf/LocalMapPath(policymap.MapName, endpointID)`). The BPF datapath uses this map at packet-processing time to allow or drop connections.\n\n---\n\n## 6. `syncPolicyMap()` applies pending policy changes to the kernel per-endpoint `policymap`.\n- `pkg/endpoint/regeneration/` \u2014 context types and triggers for endpoint regeneration.\n\n### `pkg/maps` \u2014 eBPF Map Definitions\nDefines all BPF map schemas and Go accessors:\n- `pkg/maps/policymap/` \u2014 per-endpoint policy map (`PolicyMap`) used by BPF programs to make allow/deny decisions.\n- `pkg/maps/ctmap/` \u2014 connection tracking maps.\n- `pkg/maps/lxcmap/` \u2014 endpoint (LXC) map mapping endpoint IDs to their metadata.\n- `pkg/maps/nat/` \u2014 NAT maps for SNAT/DNAT.\n- `pkg/maps/ipcache/` \u2014 global ipcache BPF map mapping IPs to security identities.\n- `pkg/maps/lbmap/` \u2014 load balancer maps for service VIP routing.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Configuration Loading\n\nConfiguration is loaded through a multi-stage pipeline using **Viper** (`github.com/spf13/viper`) as the config-binding library, with **Cobra** (`github.com/spf13/cobra`) for CLI flag parsing.\n\n**Pipeline stages (in order):**\n\n1. **Flag registration** \u2014 `InitGlobalFlags()` in `daemon/cmd/daemon_main.go` registers all CLI flags with Cobra and binds each to a Viper key via `option.BindEnv()` (`pkg/option/config.go:1361`). This also binds a `CILIUM_<FLAG>` environment variable for every flag automatically.\n\n2. **cobra.OnInitialize hooks** (registered in `daemon/cmd/root.go:66`) run before command execution:\n   - `option.InitConfig()` (`pkg/option/config.go:4215`) \u2014 reads config from the `--config-dir` directory (a directory of key=value files, compatible with Kubernetes ConfigMap volume mounts), then reads a YAML/TOML/JSON config file specified by `--config`. Uses `vp.ReadInConfig()`.\n   - `initDaemonConfig()` \u2014 populates the global `option.Config` (`*option.DaemonConfig`) from the populated Viper instance.\n   - `initLogging()` \u2014 configures log levels and output format.\n\n3. **Config formats supported:** YAML, TOML, JSON (via Viper), plus directory-based key=value files.\n\n4. **Main config struct:** `option.DaemonConfig` (`pkg/option/config.go:1401`) \u2014 a large struct with hundreds of fields covering all daemon options. The global singleton is `option.Config`. It is provided to hive cells via `cell.Provide(func() *option.DaemonConfig { return option.Config })` in `daemon/cmd/cells.go:101`.\n\n5. **Per-cell config:** Individual cells also define their own smaller config structs using `cell.Config(MyConfig{})`, which the hive framework automatically populates from Viper using struct field name matching.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Test Structure\n\nThe project uses several distinct testing approaches:\n\n### 1. Standard Go Unit Tests\nCo-located with the package they test (e.g., `pkg/policy/repository_test.go`, `pkg/option/config_test.go`). Run with `go test ./pkg/...`. These use the standard `testing` package and `github.com/stretchr/testify`, and test pure logic with no kernel dependencies.\n\n### 2. Privileged Tests\nTests that require root/kernel capabilities (loading BPF programs, creating network namespaces, etc.). They call `testutils.PrivilegedTest(t)` at the start, which skips the test unless running as root. Examples:\n- `pkg/maps/policymap/policymap_privileged_test.go` \u2014 tests actual BPF map operations.\n- `pkg/datapath/loader/tc_test.go` \u2014 tests TC program attachment.\n- `pkg/datapath/linux/probes/probes_test.go` \u2014 tests kernel feature probes.\n- `pkg/mountinfo/mountinfo_privileged_test.go` \u2014 tests filesystem mount detection.\n\n### 3. BPF / Datapath C Tests\nLocated in `bpf/tests/`. Written in C and executed via a Go test harness (`bpf/tests/bpftest/bpf_test.go`). These compile and run eBPF programs in a controlled environment to test datapath logic in isolation (e.g., conntrack, NAT, policy drop, nodeport load balancing). Example files: `bpf/tests/tc_lxc_policy_drop.c`, `bpf/tests/bpf_ct_tests.c`, `bpf/tests/conntrack_test.c`.\n\n### 4. End-to-End Tests\nLocated in `test/`. Ginkgo-based tests that run against real Kubernetes clusters or VMs via Vagrant:\n- Entry point: `test/test_suite_test.go` \u2014 bootstraps the Ginkgo suite using `github.com/onsi/ginkgo`, imports `test/k8s` and `test/runtime`.\n- `test/k8s/` \u2014 full K8s e2e tests: network policies (`net_policies.go`), services, FQDN, Hubble, BGP, bandwidth, chaos tests.\n- `test/runtime/` \u2014 runtime tests against a single Cilium node.\n- `test/controlplane/` \u2014 lighter-weight controlplane integration tests checking policy and endpoint reconciliation without a full cluster.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Network Policy Pipeline\n\nA `CiliumNetworkPolicy` travels through the following stages from CRD definition to eBPF enforcement:\n\n### Stage 1: CRD Type Definition\n**Package:** `pkg/k8s/apis/cilium.io/v2/`\n**Key file:** `cnp_types.go`\n\n`CiliumNetworkPolicy` is a Kubernetes CRD with `Spec *api.Rule` and `Specs api.Rules`. The `api.Rule` struct (from `pkg/policy/api/rule.go`) contains `EndpointSelector`, `Ingress []IngressRule`, `Egress []EgressRule`, and optional `EnableDefaultDeny`. `IngressRule` and `EgressRule` compose `IngressCommonRule`/`EgressCommonRule` (endpoint/CIDR/entity selectors) with `ToPorts []PortRule` (L4/L7 rules).\n\n### Stage 2: Kubernetes Watcher / Event Ingestion\n**Package:** `pkg/policy/k8s/`\n**Key files:** `watcher.go`, `cilium_network_policy.go`\n\nThe `policyWatcher` struct (registered via `policyK8s.Cell` in `daemon/cmd/cells.go`) watches typed K8s resource event streams for `CiliumNetworkPolicy`, `CiliumClusterwideNetworkPolicy`, `NetworkPolicy`, and `CiliumCIDRGroup` resources. On `Upsert` events, `onUpsert()` translates the K8s object to an internal `types.SlimCNP`, resolves any `CiliumCIDRGroup` references, and calls the `PolicyManager` interface to add rules to the repository.\n\n### Stage 3: Policy Repository\n**Package:** `pkg/policy/`\n**Key file:** `repository.go`\n\nThe `Repository` struct holds all active rules in `map[ruleKey]*rule`. Updates are serialized through `RepositoryChangeQueue`. When rules change, the repository increments its `revision` atomic counter and queues endpoint regeneration events through `RuleReactionQueue`, which triggers all affected endpoints to recompute their policy.\n\n### Stage 4: Endpoint Policy Computation\n**Package:** `pkg/endpoint/`, `pkg/policy/`\n**Key files:** `pkg/endpoint/policy.go`, `pkg/policy/distillery.go`\n\n`regeneratePolicy()` (`pkg/endpoint/policy.go:200`) is called for each affected endpoint. It uses the `PolicyCache` (`pkg/policy/distillery.go`) to resolve the endpoint's identity against all repository rules via `SelectorPolicy.Consume()`, producing an `EndpointPolicy` \u2014 a distilled, identity-indexed set of allow/deny rules specific to this endpoint.\n\n### Stage 5: BPF Regeneration and Policy Map Sync\n**Package:** `pkg/endpoint/`, `pkg/maps/policymap/`\n**Key files:** `pkg/endpoint/bpf.go`, `pkg/maps/policymap/policymap.go`\n\n`regenerateBPF()` (`pkg/endpoint/bpf.go:520`) rewrites BPF C header files for the endpoint and instructs the datapath loader to recompile and reload the endpoint's BPF programs. Then `syncPolicyMap()` (`pkg/endpoint/bpf.go:1333`) reconciles the desired `EndpointPolicy` against the live kernel `PolicyMap` BPF map (one per endpoint, at path `bpf/LocalMapPath(policymap.MapName, endpointID)`). The BPF datapath uses this map at packet-processing time to allow or drop connections.\n\n---\n\n## 6. # Cilium Codebase Orientation

## 1. Main Entry Point

The `cilium-agent` binary starts in `daemon/main.go`:

```go
func main() {
    agentHive := hive.New(cmd.Agent)
    cmd.Execute(cmd.NewAgentCmd(agentHive))
}
```

**Binary entry point:** `daemon/main.go:11` — `func main()`

**CLI initialization** is handled in `daemon/cmd/root.go` via `NewAgentCmd()`. This function:
- Creates a `cobra.Command` named `"cilium-agent"`
- Registers all CLI flags via `h.RegisterFlags(rootCmd.Flags())` and `InitGlobalFlags()`
- Registers cobra init hooks: `option.InitConfig()`, `initDaemonConfig()`, `initLogging()`
- The command's `Run` function calls `initEnv()`, validates config, and then `h.Run()` to start the hive

**Dependency Injection Framework:** Cilium uses **Hive** (`github.com/cilium/hive`), a custom DI framework based on `uber-go/fx`-style cells. The `hive.New()` call in `daemon/main.go` takes a root `cell.Cell` — `cmd.Agent` — and wires all components together.

**Component composition** is defined in `daemon/cmd/cells.go`. The `Agent` cell is a `cell.Module` that contains:
- `Infrastructure` — external access (Kubernetes client, pprof, gops, metrics server, Cilium REST API)
- `ControlPlane` — per-node control logic (endpoint manager, node manager, policy watcher, IPAM, services, etc.)
- `datapath.Cell` — privileged kernel-level operations (BPF maps, iptables, loader, ipcache sync)

Each sub-cell provides or consumes typed dependencies declared via `cell.Provide`, `cell.Invoke`, `cell.Config`, and struct tags (`cell.In`).

# Cilium Codebase Orientation

## 1. Main Entry Point

The `cilium-agent` binary starts in `daemon/main.go`:

```go
func main() {
    agentHive := hive.New(cmd.Agent)
    cmd.Execute(cmd.NewAgentCmd(agentHive))
}
```

**Binary entry point:** `daemon/main.go:11` — `func main()`

**CLI initialization** is handled in `daemon/cmd/root.go` via `NewAgentCmd()`. This function:
- Creates a `cobra.Command` named `"cilium-agent"`
- Registers all CLI flags via `h.RegisterFlags(rootCmd.Flags())` and `InitGlobalFlags()`
- Registers cobra init hooks: `option.InitConfig()`, `initDaemonConfig()`, `initLogging()`
- The command's `Run` function calls `initEnv()`, validates config, and then `h.Run()` to start the hive

**Dependency Injection Framework:** Cilium uses **Hive** (`github.com/cilium/hive`), a custom DI framework modeled after `uber-go/fx`-style cells. The `hive.New()` call in `daemon/main.go` takes a root `cell.Cell` — `cmd.Agent` — and wires all components together. Individual cells declare their dependencies by embedding `cell.In` in their parameter structs, and expose outputs via `cell.Provide`.

**Component composition** is defined in `daemon/cmd/cells.go`. The `Agent` cell is a `cell.Module` that contains:
- `Infrastructure` — external access (Kubernetes client, pprof, gops, metrics server, Cilium REST API socket)
- `ControlPlane` — per-node control logic (endpoint manager, node manager, policy watcher, IPAM, services, auth, BGP, etc.)
- `datapath.Cell` — privileged kernel-level operations (BPF maps, iptables, loader, ipcache sync)

---

### `pkg/policy` — Network Policy Engine

- `Repository` (`pkg/policy/repository.go:110`) — stores all active policy rules indexed by `ruleKey` and `ResourceID`. Exposes two event
